collectible - cam_desc:(26116)
cam_descid,description
6527 1990-1992. 120 film, SLR camera. Special promotional 500C/M. Available only as a kit with 80mm CF lens and A12 back.
6510 1977-1981. 120 film, 6x6cm exposures, SLR camera. Hasselblad went back to focal-plane shutters with this camera; it has a titanium foil shutter and 1/2000 sec speed.
6507 1953-1957. 120 film, 6x6cm exposures, SLR camera. Replacement for the 1600F (top speed reduced, shutter problems fixed). It's the second Hasselblad model.
6508 1948-1953. 120 film, 6x6cm exposures, SLR camera. This is the first Hasselblad model and the world's first modular design, medium format, SLR. Interchangeable lens, back, viewfinder.
6511 1981-1984. 120 film, 6x6cm exposures, SLR camera. Compared to the FC, the only modification that has been made is that the shutter stays open if the camera back is off.
6512 1985. 120 film, 6x6cm exposures, SLR camera. Compared to the FC, the only modification that has been made is that the shutter stays open if the camera back is off. This 100th (1885-
1985) Anniversary model is gold-finished.
6513 1984-1988. 120 film, 6x6cm exposures, SLR camera. Replacement for the 2000 FC/M, with added motor-winder ability.
6514 1988-1990. 120 film, 6x6cm exposures, SLR camera. These cameras were an upgrade from the 2000 FC/M; they feature a Palpas coating inside to reduce flare.
6515 1994-1997. 120 film, 6x6cm exposures, SLR camera. Entry level model. No exposure metering, shutter speed limited to 1/000.
6516 1998-2002. 120 film, 6x6cm exposures, SLR camera. Based on the 203 FE, this camera has no manual mode, the shutter speed is limited, and it has no bracketing. Not compatible with C 
and CB lenses.
3850 c1957-1996. Miniature 35mm film camera. Original Tessina, chrome version. This camera exists in several versions (chrome, gold, red and black). The regular chrome version is the least 
valuable.
3848 c1957-1996. Miniature 35mm film camera. Original Tessina, red version. Miniature 35mm film camera. This camera exists in several versions (chrome, gold, red and black). The regular chrome 
version is the least valuable version.
3849 c1957-1996. Miniature 35mm film camera. 'Tessina 35' is an updated version of a regular Tessina (Automatic).
3852 c1957-1996. Miniature 35mm film camera. 'Tessina L' is an upgraded version of a regular Tessina. This camera exists in several versions (chrome, gold, red and black). Gold, red and black 
versions are more valuable than the regular chrome one.
3851 1960. Miniature 35mm film camera. 'Tessina L' is an upgraded version of a regular Tessina. This camera exists in several versions (chrome, gold, red and black). Gold, red and black 
versions are more valuable than the regular chrome one.
3853 c1957-1996. Miniature 35mm film camera. 'Tessina L' is an upgraded version of a regular Tessina. This camera exists in several versions (chrome, gold, red and black). Gold, red and black 
versions are more valuable than the regular chrome one.
5763 c1895-1905. 13x18cm plates, tailboard, stereo camera.
5762 c1900s. 9x12 or 13x18cm plates, gate-struts camera. Some models have magazine back.
5708 1947. 35mm film, rangefinder camera.
5713 c1955. 16mm film, subminiature camera. Spring-winder.
8552 1933. 620 rollfilm camera. Same as the Six-20 Brownie Target.
8580 1905-1910. 120 film, 8x6cm exposures. Somewhat rare.
8585 1917-1925. No.101 rollfilm, stereo camera.
8592 1938-1944. 620 film, rangefinder camera, 2¼x3¼ exposures. Folding clam-shell design. Coupled electric-eye; the first camera in the world with automatic exposure setting. Rare. Some sources 
indicate there were only 719 cameras manufactured.
17707 1930-1938. 127 film camera. Several different models were manufactured.
18194 1928-1938. 6.5x9cm folding camera. Continuation of the ICA cameras.
18196 1937-1941. 120 rollfilm camera. In the UK, sold as the "Nettar 510".
17698 1936-1941. 6x9 camera. In the UK sold as the "Nettar 510/2".
17700 1929. 22x31mm rollfilm camera.
17701 1929. 22x31mm rollfilm camera. Improved "Bobette I" with better lens.
7982 1923-1925. Metal body. Very common camera.
7983 1906-1916. Folding-bed camera. Filmpacks. Before 1907 was manufactured by Rochester Optical.
7984 1906-1910. Folding-bed camera. Filmpacks. Before 1907 was manufactured by Rochester Optical.
7988 1912-1916. 3¼×4¼, 3¼×5½, 4x5, or 5x7'' exposures on Primo filmpacks, folding camera. Same as Filmplate Premo versions, but Plantograph lens are not included in this line.
7986 1906-1916. Folding, filmpacks camera.
7989 1984. 110 film pocket camera. Rubberized sides. The "Fisher-Price" name was re-used in 2000ish by the Fisher-Price Company. They produce cameras with the same name.
7990 1947-1953. 828 film camera. Collapsible bellows front.
7991 1894-1895. Extremely rare. First Kodak folding camera with combined rollfilm back.
7994 1988-2000s. 35mm film camera. Single-use.
7995 1908-1915. 18 film (or glass plates) camera.
17835 1927-1935. Plate camera.
20207 1927-1935. Plate camera. 13x18cm. Extremely rare.
18835 1927-1935. Plate camera.
20206 1927-1935. Plate camera.
20208 1927-1931. Folding plate camera.
20209 1927-1931. Folding plate camera.
20210 1927. Folding-bed rollfilm camera.
17852 1927-1931. Wooden view camera. Rare.
20212 1927-1931. Wooden view camera. Rare.
20213 1927-1931. Wooden view camera. Rare.

collectible - cam_man_desc:(325)
manURL,description
Zeiss-Ikon <br>
<img src=../icons/Logo-Zeiss-Ikon.jpg>
<br>
<br>
Check the <a href=http://collectiblend.com/forum/viewtopic.php?f=4&t=3>Approximate Timeline of Zeiss History</a> for additional information on how Zeiss cameras are listed on this web-site. 
This page 
includes only 'Zeiss Ikon AG' cameras. Also you may want to check <a href=http://collectiblend.com/forum/viewtopic.php?f=4&t=20>Zeiss Ikon sizes classification</a>.
If you are looking for Carl Zeiss lens prices, check the <a href=/Lenses/Zeiss,-Carl/>Carl Zeiss Lens Price Guide</a> or <a href=/Lenses/Zeiss,-Carl-Jena/>Carl Zeiss Jena Lens Price 
Guide</a>.
Zeiss,Carl-VEB Check the <a href=http://collectiblend.com/forum/viewtopic.php?f=4&t=3>Approximate Timeline of Zeiss History</a> for additional information on 
how Zeiss cameras are listed on this web-site. This page includes only 'VEB Carl Zeiss Jena' cameras.
Zeiss,Carl-Jena Check the <a href=http://collectiblend.com/forum/viewtopic.php?f=4&t=3>Approximate Timeline of Zeiss History</a> for additional information on 
how Zeiss cameras are listed on this web-site. This page includes only 'Carl Zeiss Jena' cameras.
Zeiss-Ikon-VEB <br><img src=http://collectiblend.com/Cameras/icons/Logo-Zeiss-Ikon.jpg><img src=http://collectiblend.com/Cameras/icons/Logo-Pentacon.jpg><br><br>

Check the <a href=http://collectiblend.com/forum/viewtopic.php?f=4&t=3>Approximate Timeline of Zeiss History</a> for additional information on how Zeiss cameras are 
listed on this web-site. This page includes cameras by 'VEB Zeiss Ikon Dresden' which became 'VEB Pentacon' later. VEB Pentacon cameras are included here too.
Asahi <center><img src=/Cameras/icons/Logo-Asahi-Pentax-AOCO.jpg>
<img src=/Cameras/icons/Logo-Asahi-Pentax-AP.jpg>
<img src=/Cameras/icons/Logo-Honeywell-Pentax-H.jpg>
<br><br><img src=/Cameras/icons/Logo-Pentax.png><br><br></center>
Originally, Asahi Pentax produced lenses for several camera manufacturers. In 1951 the company began to manufacture its own cameras. The 
first 
of these, the Asahiflex, was also the first 
Japanese 35mm SLR. The 'Pentax' trademark was acquired by Asahi from <a href=/Cameras/Zeiss-Ikon/>Zeiss Ikon</a> in 1957. Many Pentax 
cameras 
were also sold under the 'Honeywell' name, 
by the Honeywell company, which was a distributor, not a manufacturer. In 2002 Asahi company was renamed to 
<a href=/Cameras/Pentax/>Pentax</a> (see post-2002 cameras in that section). On March 31, 2008, Pentax merged with the Hoya Corporation. 
You can 
find quite a bit of information about Pentax 
SLR cameras at <a href=http://bdimitrov.de/kmp/index.html>Bojidar Dimitrov's Pentax K-Mount Page</a>.<br>
If you are looking for Asahi/Pentax collectible lens prices, check the <a href=/Lenses/Asahi/>Asahi Lens Price Guide</a>.
Berning-Robot <br>
<img src=/Cameras/icons/Logo-Robot.gif width=200><br><br>
<i>Otto Berning & Co.</i> was founded in 1934 by Hans Heinrich Berning. The company is located in Schwelm, Westphalia, Germany. It was 
produced mainly the 'Robot' line of cameras, but also security equipment for banking institutions.
Contessa-Nettel <br><center>
<img src=/Cameras/icons/Logo-Contessa-Nettel.jpg>
</center>
<br>
<br>
<i>Contessa-Nettel AG</i> was established in 1919 as a result of a merger between <a href=/Cameras/Contessa-Werke/>Contessa Camerawerke 
Drexler & Nagel</a> and <a href=/Cameras/Nettel/>Nettel Camerawerk</a>. In 1926, the company became a part of 
<a href=/Cameras/Zeiss-Ikon/>Zeiss Ikon</a>.
Compass-Cameras <br>
<center><img src=/Cameras/icons/Logo-LeCoultre.jpg></center>
<br>
<br>
<i>Compass Cameras</i> (London, UK) was a distributor of cameras manufactured by <i>LeCoultre & Cie</i> (Sentier, Switzerland). LeCoultre is a 
manufacturer of watches from 1833.
Canon <br><center>
<img src=/Cameras/icons/Logo-Kwanon.jpg><br>
<img src=/Cameras/icons/Logo-Canon.png>
<br></center>
<br>
Founded in Japan in 1930, the original company name was <i>Precision Optical Instruments Laboratory</i>. Its first commercial camera, the 
'Hansa Canon' 
featuring Nikkor lenses, was introduced in 1936. The company name was changed to 'Canon' on August 10, 1937. An excellent timeline of film 
cameras can 
be found at the <a href=http://www.canon.com/camera-museum/camera/film/chrono_1933-1955.html>Canon Camera Museum</a> web-site.<br>
If you are looking for Canon lens prices, check the <a href=/Lenses/Canon/>Canon Lens Price Guide</a>.
Ernemann <br><center>
<img border=1 src=/Cameras/icons/Logo-Ernemann-2.jpg> <img border=1 src=/Cameras/icons/Logo-Ernemann-1.jpg><br><br>
</center>
<i>Dresdner Photographische Apparate-Fabrik Ernemann & Matthias</i> was founded in 1889, and the company expanded into the camera business 
around 1891, same time when Franz Matthias left the company, probably to work on his own business - <a href=http://collectiblend.com/Cameras/Frama/>Frama</a>. In 1899 the company 
took over the "Globus" cameras manufacturer - <a href=/Cameras/Herbst-&-Firl>Ernst Herbst & Firl</a>. In 1907 
Ernemann introduced its first SLR camera, and started producing its own lenses in 1908. Ernemann became a part of the 
newly-formed <a href=/Cameras/Zeiss-Ikon/>Zeiss Ikon</a> company in 1926.
OPL-(Foca) <br><img src=/Cameras/icons/Logo-FOCA.jpg><br><br>
The <i>OPL</i> (Société <b>O</b>ptique et <b>P</b>récision de <b>L</b>evallois) company was founded in 1919 in France, and began manufacturing 
cameras in 1945. In 1964 it was merged with SOM company, and ceased its camera production operations in 1967.
Royer <br><img src=/Cameras/icons/Logo-Royer.jpg><br><br>
Royer company (officially SITO: Société Industrielle de Technique Optique) was founded in 1947 by <i>René Royer</i>. Royer is believed to have 
bought some of its model designs from <a href=/Cameras/Photorex/>Photorex</a> and <a href=/Cameras/Atoms/>Atoms</a> when these companies went 
bankrupt.
Bilora-(Kurbi-&-Niggeloh) <br><img src=/Cameras/icons/Logo-Bilora.jpg><br><br>
<i>Bilora</i> was founded in 1909 in Germany (as <i>Kürbi & Niggeloh</i>), although it didnt enter the camera market until 1935. Camera 
production ceased around 1975, but the company still manufactures other photographic equipment.
Gevaert <br><img src=/Cameras/icons/Logo-Gevaert.jpg><br><br>
<i>L. Gevaert & Cie</i> was founded in 1894 in Antwerp, Belgium, distributing Gevaert cameras produced by other manufacturers. In 1964, 
Gevaert merged with the <a href=/Cameras/AGFA/>Agfa</a> and Bayer companies.
Kilfitt <br><img src=/Cameras/icons/Logo-Kilfitt.jpg><br><br>
<i>Killfitt</i> was a private contracting company started by <i>Heinz Killfitt</i>. Amongst his other camera achievements, Killfitt designed 
the Robot camera, which he sold to <a href=/Cameras/Berning-Robot/>Berning</a>, and the Mecaflex which he sold to <a 
href=/Cameras/Metz/>Metz</a>. However, when Metz bailed out of the deal, Mecaflex cameras were produced in Monaco by <a 
href=/Cameras/SEROA/>S.E.R.O.A.</a>
Leonar-KW <br><img src=/Cameras/icons/Logo-Leonar.jpg><br><br>
Originally established as <a href=/Cameras/Luttke-&-Arndt/>Dr. Lüttke & Arndt</a>, this company was located in Wandsbek, Germany. When <i>Dr. 
Lüttke</i> was replaced by <i>Ernst Löwengard</i> in 1905, the company became known as <i>Leonar-Werke Arndt & Löwengard</i>. In 1964 the 
company merged with <a href=/Cameras/AGFA/>Agfa</a>.
Luttke-&-Arndt The <i>Lüttke & Arndt</i> company was founded in 1895 in Germany. Camera manufacturing wasn't their main business and they offered only a few 
models. The company became <a href=/Cameras/Leonar-KW/>Leonar-Werke Arndt & Löwengard</a> in 1905.
Keystone <center><br><img src=/Cameras/icons/Logo-Keystone-Shield.jpg><br><br><img src=/Cameras/icons/Logo-Keystone.jpg><br><br></center>
<i>Keystone Camera Company</i> was established in c1919 in Boston, Massachussets. It was acquired by <i>Berkey Photo Inc</i> in 1966, and 
then sold again in 1978. During that timeframe the company was known as <i>Berkey Keystone</i>. It manufactured cine and photo cameras until 
1991 when it filed for bankruptcy. In the same year Keystone was sold to the <a href=/Cameras/Concord-Cameras/>Concord Camera Company</a>.
Krugener <center><br><img src=/Cameras/icons/Logo-Krugener.jpg><br><br></center>
<i>Dr. Rudolf Krügener, Photochemisches Laboratorium und Fabrik photographischer Apparate Frankfurt-Bockenheim</i> was a company established 
in Frankfurt, Germany in 1888. The founder, <i>Rudolf Krügener</i>, was also a manager at <a href=/Cameras/AGFA/>Agfa</a>'s Frankfurt branch. 
In 1909, Krügener merged with several other companies to become <a href=/Cameras/ICA/>Ica A.G.</a>
Kochmann <center><br><img src=/Cameras/icons/Logo-Kochman.jpg><br><br></center>
<i>Franz Kochmann Fabrik photographischer Apparate</i> was founded by Franz Kochmann in 1921 in Dresden, Germany. In 1938, Kochmann left 
the company, and it was 
taken over, operating first as <i>Korellewerke KG</i>, and then, after a name change a year later, as <i>Korelle-Werk G.H. Brandtmann & 
Co.</i> In 1948, the 
company was taken over by the East German <a href=/Cameras/Wefo/>VEB WEFO</a>, which later merged into <a href=/Cameras/Welta/>VEB 
Welta-Kamerawerke</a>. The 
last Korelle cameras were manufactured in 1952.

collectible - lens_desc:(525)
lens_descid,description
1 Somewhat rare. Focuses to 1m (3'4''). A lot of fakes made from the bayonet-mount lenses.
2 Somewhat rare. Focuses to 1m (3'4''). Only 577 lenses were manufactured. A lot of fakes made from the bayonet-mount lenses.
3 Leica I (A) lens. Serial number under 100000 or no number at all.
4 Lens for the British Navy (PATT engraving). Uncoated. Rare.
5 Lens for Leica IIIg. Excellent lens review can be found at <a href=http://www.kenrockwell.com/leica/50mm-f28.htm>kenrockwell.com</a>.
6 Nickel or chrome. Pin release or domed release.
7 Nickel is less valuable than the chrome.
9 1950. Prototype of the later Summicron series. Thorium-oxide element, f22. Rare.
10 1960-1963. Reportedly only 548 screw-mount lenses were manufactured. Order number is 11014 or SOWGE.
11 Black/nickel is slightly less valuable than black/chrome. Full or partial chrome focus ring.
97 c1966. Rare, reportedly only 100 lenses were manufactured. MVX shutter.
98 Adapted in Contax RF mount.
99 1929-1939. Rare, only 116 lenses were manufactured. Adapted for M42.
100 1968. Large format. Unique prototype for Linhof.
102 Somewhat rare.
103 c1970. Mirror lens. Adapters to other mounts (M42, Canon EOS, Nikon F, Pentax K, Exakta, etc) are widely available.
Also known as Prakticar. Only 60 lenses were manufactured.
104 Synchro-Compur shutter, Linhof Technika 4x5" lens board.
105 Brass taking lens for waterhouse stops, rack and pinion focusing.
106 Lens for M39 SLR cameras.
107 Rare prototype lens.
2102 200 lenses were manufactured.
3742 Only two sample lenses were manufactured.
3749 About 260 lenses were manufactured.
3769 c1880. Lens needs to be filled with water.
3827 1979. Lenses for E49 filter never went into production.
3829 1977. Gold-plated lens for M4-2 Gold Edition.
3831 1959. 'Bw' engraving.
3832 1995. Only 12 lenses were manufactured in Canada as samples.
1701 1949. Only 14 lens were manufactured.
3833 1988-2009. SN from c3475151. Order no.11339.

10 357 0 5 1264266237 Ferrania: Lince 3 My first 35mm camera!
Bought brand new in 1963, I had this camera for years and years, and used it regularly until ca 1973 when I got a Robot Royal 36, which in turn was replaced by a Canon SLR.

A reliable, scale-focusing little camera with limited capabilities, no real long shutterspeeds, and a 45,, f:2,8 lens it was used as the basis for B/W negatives in the school darkroom, and later together with a Gossen Sixtino exposure meter for color slides (Ferrania of course!) and color negatives.

This was a reliable, light little workhorse, only problem that ever developed was  a problem with the film transport, giving overlapped negatives, this was fixed by a local repair shop, shortly before I got my Robot.

Kept the camera as part of a modest collection until the early 1990's when it was sold off cheaply to another collector.
The camera is missed and the picrtures will be revived when my slide and negative collection gets scanned later this year!

collectible_forum - phpbb_posts:(4866)

3 3 4 2 0 155.70.39.45 1202245153 0 1 1 0 1 Zeiss Company History Most &quot;Zeiss&quot; cameras were produced at the beginning of last century by [url=http&#58;//collectiblend&#46;com/Cameras/Zeiss-Ikon/:1u2jie5s]Zeiss Ikon AG[/url:1u2jie5s], which was created in 1926 by the Carl Zeiss Foundation as a merge of several companies, including ICA, Contessa-Nettel, Ernemann and C.P. Goerz. In 1945 (after the war) Zeiss Ikon was split into two parts, the Eastern and Western. 

The Western part was licensed to produce cameras under the same Zeiss Ikon name in the western world, so its cameras are also listed under [url=http&#58;//collectiblend&#46;com/Cameras/Zeiss-Ikon/:1u2jie5s]Zeiss Ikon[/url:1u2jie5s]. Unfortunately, it ceased production in 1972, and was split up and sold to several other companies. 

The Eastern part, VEB Zeiss Ikon, also produced some cameras under the same names, for distribution in the part of Eastern Europe occupied by the USSR. This company is listed in this catalogue as [url=http&#58;//collectiblend&#46;com/Cameras/Zeiss-Ikon-VEB/:1u2jie5s]Zeiss Ikon VEB[/url:1u2jie5s]. This is a matter of individual preference, since some Price Guides suggest listing this company under the Pentacon name in virtue of the fact that it was merged into VEB Pentacon later.


Now, with respect to the other part of Zeiss world: [url=http&#58;//collectiblend&#46;com/Cameras/Zeiss,Carl-Jena/:1u2jie5s]Carl Zeiss Jena[/url:1u2jie5s], which was producing cameras as early as 18th century. After the war this company was divided into two parts as well: Eastern [url=http&#58;//collectiblend&#46;com/Cameras/Zeiss,Carl-VEB/:1u2jie5s]VEB Carl Zeiss[/url:1u2jie5s] (known for Werra cameras), and western Carl Zeiss (which was not producing cameras). After a long history, these two companies are now back together, operating under the name of Carl Zeiss.

[url=http&#58;//collectiblend&#46;com/Cameras/zeiss_history2&#46;png:1u2jie5s][thumb=&quot;533,456,http&#58;//collectiblend&#46;com/Cameras/zeiss_history2&#46;png Zeiss History Timeline &#40;Carl Zeiss, Zeiss Ikon&#41;&quot;:1u2jie5s][/thumb:1u2jie5s][/url:1u2jie5s]
The arrow pointing to the &quot;year&quot; line shows the point at which the company was divided, created or merged with other company. 77 31 2 2 0 97.118.72.30 1228873389 0 1 1 1 1 Re: Nkon D200 Body Dear RPC,
This site deals with collectible film cameras only. Unfortunately digitals ones are not presented here.
But here is my personal opinion about the question #3, since I am a long-time user of digital SLRs: if the D200-&gt;D300 will cost you more than $300, don't. I think the main advantage of D300 is a CMOS sensor vs a CCD one in D200, hence the more clarity (not because of extra 2mp). Everything else is not a big step up, your friend is right. 4854 226 5 3055 0 76.117.59.160 1454862338 0 1 1 1 1 Re: Yashica: Yashica MG-1 The MG-1 was released in 1975 and discontinued in 1980. It has an electronic controlled leaf-type shutter providing continuous variable speeds from 1/500 of a second to 2 seconds approximately. It also has built-in self-timer, direct X contact shoe (shutter speed automatically sets at 1/30 sec. when the Auto Lever is adjusted to 'flash'), and a 45mm f/2.8 Yashinon lens composed of four elements in three groups.This is one of the last metal cameras before the &quot;Plastic Age&quot;. The Yashica MG-1 is a full size rangefinder. The automatic functions is a feature which makes it easy to use. The almost total lack of manual control is no doubt a limitation. It is achieved with a higher cost. The ASA range is from 25 to 800 and the camera has a one action 180 degree film advance thumb lever. The viewfinder uses red and yellow exposure indicator arrows visible through the eye piece. Using the viewfinder/rangefinder focus is secured by rotating the focusing ring and superimposing two images in the focusing spot at the center of the viewfinder field. The focus range is from 1 meter (3.3ft) to infinity. The power was provided by a 5.6V mercury battery (Eveready E164 Mallory PX32 or equivalent) but now can use a 6V Alkaline A32, A164, or A32PX single battery. The Yashica MG-1 was the last in the respected Electro 35 series, which began in 1966. Its the only camera in the series that doesnt have Electro 35 stamped somewhere on the top plate. 403fbe13b48c0ed68062fac0006222c8 0 zhluze3v 1 0 0 0 0 1 0 0
